			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Nazi Party</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the German Nazi Party that existed from 1920 to 1945. For the ideology, see <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>. For other Nazi Parties, see <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Nazi Party (disambiguation)">Nazi Party (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>National Socialist German Workers' Party</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Nationalsozialistische_Deutsche_Arbeiterpartei.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/De-Nationalsozialistische_Deutsche_Arbeiterpartei.ogg" class="internal" title="De-Nationalsozialistische Deutsche Arbeiterpartei.ogg"><span class="unicode" lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Nationalsozialistische Deutsche Arbeiterpartei</i></span></a></span>&#160;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:De-Nationalsozialistische_Deutsche_Arbeiterpartei.ogg" title="File:De-Nationalsozialistische Deutsche Arbeiterpartei.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>, abbreviated <b>NSDAP</b>), commonly referred to in English as the <b>Nazi Party</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/iː/ or /ɪ/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>), was a <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> political party in Germany that was active between 1920 and 1945 and practised the ideology of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>. Its precursor, the <a href="/wiki/German_Workers%27_Party" title="German Workers' Party">German Workers' Party</a> (<i>Deutsche Arbeiterpartei</i>; DAP), existed from 1919 to 1920.</p>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_German_Reich_(1935%E2%80%931945).svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the German Reich (1935–1945).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Flag_of_the_German_Reich_%281935%E2%80%931945%29.svg/16px-Flag_of_the_German_Reich_%281935%E2%80%931945%29.svg.png" width="16" height="10" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Flag_of_the_German_Reich_%281935%E2%80%931945%29.svg/24px-Flag_of_the_German_Reich_%281935%E2%80%931945%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Flag_of_the_German_Reich_%281935%E2%80%931945%29.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_German_Reich_%281935%E2%80%931945%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Nazi_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Portal:Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany portal</a></i></li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">

<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Nazism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Nazism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Nazism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>

</table>
<p>The party emerged from the <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism" title="German nationalism">German nationalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Freikorps" title="Freikorps">Freikorps</a></i> paramilitary culture, which fought against the <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">communist</a> uprisings in post-<a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-stormtroopers_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stormtroopers-6">[6]</a></sup> The party was created as a means to draw workers away from communism and into <i><a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch" class="mw-redirect" title="Völkisch">völkisch</a></i> nationalism.<sup id="cite_ref-stormtroopers2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stormtroopers2-7">[7]</a></sup> Initially, Nazi political strategy focused on <a href="/wiki/Big_business" title="Big business">anti-big business</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">anti-bourgeois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-capitalism" title="Anti-capitalism">anti-capitalist</a> rhetoric, although such aspects were later downplayed in order to gain the support of industrial entities, and in the 1930s the party's focus shifted to <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">anti-Semitic</a> and anti-Marxist themes.<sup id="cite_ref-mcdonough_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcdonough-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nazism_and_race" title="Nazism and race">Pseudo-scientific racism</a> theories were central to Nazism. The Nazis propagated the idea of a "people's community" (<i><a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">Volksgemeinschaft</a></i>). Their aim was to unite "racially desirable" Germans as national comrades, while excluding those deemed either to be political dissidents, physically or intellectually inferior, or of a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_races" title="Foreign races">foreign race</a> (<i>Fremdvölkische</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Wildt2012_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wildt2012-9">[9]</a></sup> The Nazis sought to improve the stock of the Germanic people through racial purity and <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a>, broad social welfare programs, and a <a href="/wiki/Collectivism" title="Collectivism">collective</a> subordination of individual rights, which could be sacrificed for the good of the state and the "<a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan</a> <a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a>". To maintain the supposed purity and strength of the Aryan race, the Nazis sought to <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">exterminate</a> <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Romani</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a> along with the vast majority of other <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavs</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Physical_disability" title="Physical disability">physically</a> and <a href="/wiki/Developmental_disability" title="Developmental disability">mentally handicapped</a>. They imposed exclusionary segregation on <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_homosexuals_in_Nazi_Germany_and_the_Holocaust" title="Persecution of homosexuals in Nazi Germany and the Holocaust">homosexuals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazism_and_race" title="Nazism and race">Africans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, and political opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-massachusetts_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-massachusetts-10">[10]</a></sup> The persecution reached its climax when the party-controlled German state organized the systematic murder of approximately six million Jews and five million people from the other targeted groups, in what has become known as <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>.</p>
<p>The party's leader since 1921, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, was appointed <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">Chancellor of Germany</a> by President <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a> on 30 January 1933. Hitler rapidly established a <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarian</a> regime<sup id="cite_ref-totalitarianism_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalitarianism-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-totalitarianism3_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalitarianism3-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-totalitarianism4_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalitarianism4-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-totalitarianism5_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalitarianism5-14">[14]</a></sup> known as the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Third Reich</a>. Following the defeat of the Third Reich at the conclusion of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in Europe, the party was "declared to be illegal" by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied powers</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> who carried out <a href="/wiki/Denazification" title="Denazification">denazification</a> in the years after the war.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The term "Nazi" derives from the name given in German to a party member <i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de">Nationalsozialist</span></i> (<small>German pronunciation:</small> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[naʦi̯oˈnaːlzoʦi̯aˌlɪst]</a></span>) and was coined in response to the German term <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Sozi" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:Sozi">Sozi</a></i> (pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/zoːtsi/</span>), an abbreviation of <i>Sozialdemokrat</i> (Member of the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party of Germany</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-SZspeak_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SZspeak-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mautner_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mautner-17">[17]</a></sup> Members of the party referred to themselves as <i>Nationalsozialisten</i> (National Socialists), rarely as Nazis. The term <i>Parteigenosse</i> (party member) was commonly used among Nazis, with the feminine form <i>Parteigenossin</i> used when it was appropriate.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term was in use before the rise of the party as a colloquial and derogatory word for a backward <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasant</a>, characterising an awkward and clumsy person. It derived from <i>Ignaz</i>, being a shortened version of <a href="/wiki/Ignatius" title="Ignatius">Ignatius</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Copenhagen_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Copenhagen-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-etymonline_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-20">[20]</a></sup> a common name in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, the area from which the Nazis emerged. Opponents seized on this and shortened the party's name in intentional association to the long-time existing 'Sozi' to the dismissive "Nazi".<sup id="cite_ref-etymonline_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sourcebook_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sourcebook-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1933, when <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> assumed power of the German government, usage of the designation "Nazi" diminished in Germany, although Austrian anti-Nazis continued to use the term derogatorily.<sup id="cite_ref-Mautner_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mautner-17">[17]</a></sup> The use of "<a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>," and "Nazi regime," was popularised by anti-Nazis and German exiles abroad. Thereafter, the term spread into other languages and eventually was brought back to Germany after the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-Sourcebook_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sourcebook-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Origins_and_early_existence:_1918–1923"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_early_existence:_1918.E2.80.931923">Origins and early existence: 1918–1923</span></h3>
<p>The party grew out of smaller political groups with a nationalist orientation that formed in the last years of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War&#160;I</a>. In 1918, a league called the <i>Freien Arbeiterausschuss für einen guten Frieden</i> (Free Workers' Committee for a good Peace)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882-22">[22]</a></sup> was created in <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a>, Germany. On 7 March 1918, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Drexler" title="Anton Drexler">Anton Drexler</a>, an avid German nationalist, formed a branch of this league in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882-22">[22]</a></sup> Drexler was a local locksmith who had been a member of the militarist <a href="/wiki/Fatherland_Party_(Germany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatherland Party (Germany)">Fatherland Party</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer199134_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer199134-23">[23]</a></sup> during World War I, and was bitterly opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Armistice_with_Germany_(Compi%C3%A8gne)" class="mw-redirect" title="Armistice with Germany (Compiègne)">armistice</a> of November 1918 and the revolutionary upheavals that followed. Drexler followed the views of militant nationalists of the day, such as opposing the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>, having <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitic</a>, anti-monarchist and anti-Marxist views, as well as believing in the superiority of Germans whom they claimed to be part of the <a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan</a> "<a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a>" (<i>Herrenvolk</i>). However, he also accused international capitalism of being a Jewish-dominated movement and denounced capitalists for war profiteering in World War I.<sup id="cite_ref-Spector.2C_pp._137_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spector.2C_pp._137-24">[24]</a></sup> Drexler saw the political violence and instability in Germany the result of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a> being out-of-touch with the masses, especially the lower classes.<sup id="cite_ref-Spector.2C_pp._137_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spector.2C_pp._137-24">[24]</a></sup> Drexler emphasized the need for a synthesis of <i>völkisch</i> nationalism with a form of economic <a href="/wiki/Preussentum_und_Sozialismus" title="Preussentum und Sozialismus">socialism</a>, in order to create a popular nationalist-oriented workers' movement that could challenge the rise of Communism and <a href="/wiki/Internationalism_(politics)" title="Internationalism (politics)">internationalist politics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-university6_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-university6-25">[25]</a></sup> These were all well-known themes popular with various <a href="/wiki/Weimar_paramilitary_groups" title="Weimar paramilitary groups">Weimar paramilitary groups</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Freikorps" title="Freikorps">Freikorps</a></i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NSDAP-Logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/NSDAP-Logo.svg/170px-NSDAP-Logo.svg.png" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/NSDAP-Logo.svg/255px-NSDAP-Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/NSDAP-Logo.svg/340px-NSDAP-Logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NSDAP-Logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nazi Party badge emblem</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Drexler's movement did receive attention and support from some influential figures. Supporter <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Eckhart" class="mw-redirect" title="Dietrich Eckhart">Dietrich Eckhart</a> brought military figure Count <a href="/wiki/Felix_Graf_von_Bothmer" title="Felix Graf von Bothmer">Felix Graf von Bothmer</a>, a prominent supporter of the concept of "national socialism", to address the movement.<sup id="cite_ref-TheodoreFred_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheodoreFred-26">[26]</a></sup> Later in 1918, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Harrer" title="Karl Harrer">Karl Harrer</a> (a journalist and member of the <a href="/wiki/Thule_Society" title="Thule Society">Thule Society</a>), convinced Drexler and several others to form the <i><a href="/wiki/Politischer_Arbeiterzirkel" class="mw-redirect" title="Politischer Arbeiterzirkel">Politischer Arbeiterzirkel</a></i> (Political Workers' Circle).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882-22">[22]</a></sup> The members met periodically for discussions with themes of nationalism and racism directed against the Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882-22">[22]</a></sup> In December 1918, Drexler decided that a new political party should be formed, based on the political principles that he endorsed, by combining his branch of the Workers' Committee for a good Peace with the Political Workers' Circle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200882-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-university7_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-university7-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 January 1919, Drexler created a new political party and proposed it be named the "German Socialist Workers' Party", but Harrer objected to the term "socialist"; so the term was removed and the party was named the <a href="/wiki/German_Workers%27_Party" title="German Workers' Party">German Workers' Party</a> (<i>Deutsche Arbeiterpartei</i>, DAP).<sup id="cite_ref-carlsten_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carlsten-28">[28]</a></sup> To ease concerns among potential middle-class supporters, Drexler made clear that unlike Marxists, the party supported the middle-class, and that its socialist policy was meant to give <a href="/wiki/Social_welfare_provision" class="mw-redirect" title="Social welfare provision">social welfare</a> to German citizens deemed part of the Aryan race.<sup id="cite_ref-Spector.2C_pp._137_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spector.2C_pp._137-24">[24]</a></sup> They became one of many <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch_movement" title="Völkisch movement"><i>völkisch</i> movements</a> that existed in Germany. Like other <i>völkisch</i> groups, the DAP advocated the belief that through <a href="/wiki/Profit-sharing" class="mw-redirect" title="Profit-sharing">profit-sharing</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Socialization_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialization (economics)">socialisation</a> Germany should become a unified "people's community" (<i>Volksgemeinschaft</i>) rather than a society divided along class and party lines.<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich-29">[29]</a></sup> This ideology was explicitly antisemitic. As early as 1920, the party was raising money by selling a tobacco called <i>Anti-Semit</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-weidenfeld_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weidenfeld-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nsdap01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Nsdap01.jpg/170px-Nsdap01.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Nsdap01.jpg/255px-Nsdap01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Nsdap01.jpg/340px-Nsdap01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="658" data-file-height="834" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nsdap01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
NSDAP membership book</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the outset, the DAP was opposed to non-nationalist political movements, especially on the left, including the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party of Germany</a> (SPD) and the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">Communist Party of Germany</a> (KPD). Members of the DAP saw themselves as fighting against "<a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevism</a>" and anyone considered a part of or aiding so-called "<a href="/wiki/Antisemitic_canard" title="Antisemitic canard">international Jewry</a>". The DAP was also deeply opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Versailles_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Versailles Treaty">Versailles Treaty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer199133_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer199133-31">[31]</a></sup> The DAP did not attempt to make itself public, and meetings were kept in relative secrecy, with public speakers discussing what they thought of Germany's present <a href="/wiki/State_of_affairs_(Sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="State of affairs (Sociology)">state of affairs</a>, or writing to like-minded societies in Northern Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The DAP was a comparatively small group with fewer than 60 members.<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich-29">[29]</a></sup> Nevertheless, it attracted the attention of the German authorities, who were suspicious of any organisation that appeared to have subversive tendencies. In July 1919 while stationed in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>, army <i><a href="/wiki/Gefreiter" title="Gefreiter">Gefreiter</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> was appointed a <i>Verbindungsmann</i> (intelligence agent) of an <i>Aufklärungskommando</i> (reconnaissance unit) of the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichswehr" title="Reichswehr">Reichswehr</a></i> (army) by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Mayr" title="Karl Mayr">Captain Mayr</a> the head of the <i>Education and Propaganda Department</i> (Dept Ib/P) in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>. Hitler was assigned to influence other soldiers and to infiltrate the DAP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200871.E2.80.9382_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200871.E2.80.9382-32">[32]</a></sup> While attending a party meeting on 12 September 1919, Hitler became involved in a heated argument with a visitor, Professor Baumann, who questioned the soundness of <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Feder" title="Gottfried Feder">Gottfried Feder</a>'s arguments against capitalism and proposed that Bavaria should break away from <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> and found a new South German nation with <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>. In vehemently attacking the man's arguments, he made an impression on the other party members with his oratory skills and, according to Hitler, the "professor" left the hall acknowledging unequivocal defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200875_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200875-33">[33]</a></sup> Drexler encouraged him to join the DAP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200875_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200875-33">[33]</a></sup> On the orders of his army superiors, Hitler applied to join the party,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003170_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003170-34">[34]</a></sup> and within a week was accepted as party member 555 (the party began counting membership at 500 to give the impression they were a much larger party).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200875.2C_76_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200875.2C_76-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitcham199667_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitcham199667-36">[36]</a></sup> Among the party's earlier members were <a href="/wiki/Ernst_R%C3%B6hm" title="Ernst Röhm">Ernst Röhm</a> of the Army's District Command VII; well-to-do journalist <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Eckart" title="Dietrich Eckart">Dietrich Eckart</a>, who has been called the spiritual father of National Socialism.;<sup id="cite_ref-Blamires2006_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blamires2006-37">[37]</a></sup> then <a href="/wiki/University_of_Munich" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Munich">University of Munich</a> student <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Hess" title="Rudolf Hess">Rudolf Hess</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer199143_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer199143-38">[38]</a></sup> <i>Freikorps</i> soldier <a href="/wiki/Hans_Frank" title="Hans Frank">Hans Frank</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">Alfred Rosenberg</a>, often credited as the philosopher of the movement. All were later prominent in the Nazi regime.<sup id="cite_ref-RisenFallofN.Germanypg.88_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RisenFallofN.Germanypg.88-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png/220px-Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png/330px-Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png/440px-Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png 2x" data-file-width="718" data-file-height="475" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hitler%27s_DAP_membership_card.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hitler's membership card in the DAP (later NSDAP)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hitler later claimed to be the seventh party member (he was in fact the seventh executive member of the party's central committee;<sup id="cite_ref-WarningfromHistory_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WarningfromHistory-40">[40]</a></sup> he would later wear the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Party_Badge" title="Golden Party Badge">Golden Party Badge</a> number one). Anton Drexler drafted a letter to Hitler in 1940, which was never sent that contradicts Hitler's later claim:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>No one knows better than you yourself, my Führer, that you were never the seventh member of the party, but at best the seventh member of the committee... And a few years ago I had to complain to a party office that your first proper membership card of the DAP, bearing the signatures of Schüssler and myself, was falsified, with the number 555 being erased and number 7 entered.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Hitler's first DAP speech was held in the <a href="/wiki/Hofbr%C3%A4ukeller" title="Hofbräukeller">Hofbräukeller</a> on 16 October 1919. He was the second speaker of the evening and spoke to 111 people.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Hitler later declared that this was when he realised he could really "make a good speech".<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reich-29">[29]</a></sup> At first, Hitler spoke only to relatively small groups, but his considerable oratory and propaganda skills were appreciated by the party leadership. With the support of Anton Drexler, Hitler became chief of propaganda for the party in early 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-RisenFallofN.Germanypg.89_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RisenFallofN.Germanypg.89-43">[43]</a></sup> Hitler began to make the party more public, and organised their biggest meeting yet of 2000 people, on 24 February 1920 in the <i><a href="/wiki/Hofbr%C3%A4uhaus_am_Platzl" title="Hofbräuhaus am Platzl">Staatliches Hofbräuhaus in München</a></i>. Such was the significance of this particular move in publicity that <a href="/wiki/Karl_Harrer" title="Karl Harrer">Harrer</a> resigned from the party in disagreement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer199136_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer199136-44">[44]</a></sup> It was in this speech that Hitler enunciated the <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Program#German_Party_program" title="National Socialist Program">twenty-five points of the German Workers' Party manifesto</a> that had been drawn up by Drexler, Feder, and Hitler.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer199137_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer199137-45">[45]</a></sup> Through these points he gave the organisation a much bolder stratagem<sup id="cite_ref-RisenFallofN.Germanypg.89_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RisenFallofN.Germanypg.89-43">[43]</a></sup> with a clear foreign policy (abrogation of The Treaty of Versailles, a <a href="/wiki/German_question" class="mw-redirect" title="German question">Greater Germany</a>, Eastern expansion, exclusion of Jews from citizenship), and among his specific points were: confiscation of <a href="/wiki/War_profiteering" title="War profiteering">war profits</a>, abolition of unearned incomes, the State to share profits of land, and land for national needs to be taken away without compensation.<sup id="cite_ref-HistoryoftheModernWorldpg.133_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryoftheModernWorldpg.133-46">[46]</a></sup> In general, the manifesto was <a href="/wiki/Antisemitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Antisemitic">antisemitic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-capitalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-capitalist">anti-capitalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">anti-democratic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Marxist">anti-Marxist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">anti-liberal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.42_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.42-47">[47]</a></sup> To increase its appeal to larger segments of the population, on 24 February 1920, the same day as Hitler's <i>Hofbräuhaus</i> speech, the DAP changed its name to the <i>Nationalsozialistische Deutsche Arbeiterpartei</i> (National Socialist German Workers' Party or Nazi Party).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200887_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200887-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZentnerBed.C3.BCrftig1997629_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZentnerBed.C3.BCrftig1997629-49">[49]</a></sup> The word "Socialist" was added by the party's executive committee, over Hitler's objections, in order to help appeal to left-wing workers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMitcham199668_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitcham199668-50">[50]</a></sup> Although the Nazis considered themselves to be socialists, British historian <a href="/wiki/Richard_J._Evans" title="Richard J. Evans">Richard J. Evans</a> states "...it would be wrong to see Nazism as a form of, or an outgrowth of, socialism."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1920, the Nazi Party officially announced that only persons of "pure Aryan descent [<i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de">rein arischer Abkunft</span></i>]" could become party members; if the person had a spouse, the spouse also had to be a "racially pure" Aryan. Party members could not be related either directly or indirectly to a so-called "non-Aryan".<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrenreich2007_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrenreich2007-52">[52]</a></sup> Even before it had become legally forbidden by the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Laws" title="Nuremberg Laws">Nuremberg Laws</a> in 1935, the Nazis banned sexual relations and marriages between party members and Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-Weikart2009_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weikart2009-53">[53]</a></sup> Party members found guilty of <i><a href="/wiki/Rassenschande" title="Rassenschande">Rassenschande</a></i> (racial defilement) were persecuted heavily, some members were even sentenced to death.<sup id="cite_ref-Gordon1984_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon1984-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hitler quickly became the party's most active orator. Appearing in public as a speaker thirty-one times within the first year after his self-discovery,<sup id="cite_ref-Fest1972_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fest1972-55">[55]</a></sup> crowds began to flock to hear his speeches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200889_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200889-56">[56]</a></sup> Hitler always spoke about the same subjects: the Treaty of Versailles and <a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_question" class="mw-redirect" title="The Jewish question">the Jewish question</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.42_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.42-47">[47]</a></sup> This deliberate technique and effective publicising of the party contributed significantly to his early success,<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.42_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.42-47">[47]</a></sup> about which a contemporary poster wrote 'Since Herr Hitler is a brilliant speaker, we can hold out the prospect of an extremely exciting evening'.<sup id="cite_ref-hilterbewegung_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hilterbewegung-57">[57]</a></sup> Over the following months, the party continued to attract new members,<sup id="cite_ref-WarningfromHistory_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WarningfromHistory-40">[40]</a></sup> while remaining too small to have any real significance in German politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer199138_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer199138-58">[58]</a></sup> By the end of the year, party membership was recorded at 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200889_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200889-56">[56]</a></sup> many of whom Hitler and Röhm had brought into the party personally, or for whom Hitler's oratory had been their reason for joining.<sup id="cite_ref-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.40_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Face_of_the_3rd_Reichpg.40-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hitler's talent as an orator, and his ability to draw new members, combined with his characteristic ruthlessness, soon made him the dominant figure. However, while Hitler and Eckart were on a fundraising trip to Berlin in June 1921, a mutiny broke out within the party in Munich. Members of its executive committee wanted to merge with the rival <a href="/wiki/German_Socialist_Party" title="German Socialist Party">German Socialist Party</a> (DSP).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008100.2C_101_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008100.2C_101-60">[60]</a></sup> Upon returning to Munich on 11 July, Hitler angrily tendered his resignation. The committee members realised that his resignation would mean the end of the party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008102_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008102-61">[61]</a></sup> Hitler announced he would rejoin on the condition that he would replace Drexler as party chairman, and that the party headquarters would remain in Munich.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008103_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008103-62">[62]</a></sup> The committee agreed, and he rejoined the party on 26 July as member 3,680. Hitler continued to face some opposition within the NSDAP: Opponents of Hitler had <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Esser" title="Hermann Esser">Hermann Esser</a> expelled from the party, and they printed 3,000 copies of a pamphlet attacking Hitler as a traitor to the party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008103_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008103-62">[62]</a></sup> In the following days, Hitler spoke to several packed houses and defended himself and Esser, to thunderous applause.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200883.2C_103_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200883.2C_103-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>His strategy proved successful, and at a special party congress on 29 July 1921, he replaced Drexler as party chairman, by a vote of 533<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>to<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>1.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200883.2C_103_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200883.2C_103-63">[63]</a></sup> The committee was dissolved, and Hitler was granted nearly absolute powers as the party's sole leader.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw200883.2C_103_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw200883.2C_103-63">[63]</a></sup> This was a post he would hold for the remainder of his life. Hitler soon acquired the title <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer</a></i> ("leader") and, after a series of sharp internal conflicts, it was accepted that the party would be governed by the <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrerprinzip" title="Führerprinzip">Führerprinzip</a></i> ("leader principle"). Under this principle, the party was a highly centralized entity that functioned strictly from the top down, with Hitler at the apex as the party's absolute leader. Hitler saw the party as a revolutionary organization, whose aim was the overthrow of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>, which he saw as controlled by the socialists, Jews and the "<a href="/wiki/Stab-in-the-back_legend" class="mw-redirect" title="Stab-in-the-back legend">November criminals</a>" who had betrayed the German soldiers in 1918. The <a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">SA</a> ("storm troopers", also known as "Brownshirts") were founded as a party militia in 1921, and began violent attacks on other parties.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg/220px-Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg/330px-Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg/440px-Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1180" data-file-height="1180" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Erstausgabe_von_Mein_Kampf.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i> in its first edition cover.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For Hitler, the twin goals of the party were always German nationalist expansionism and <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a>. These two goals were fused in his mind by his belief that Germany's external enemies&#160;– Britain, France and the Soviet Union&#160;– were controlled by the Jews, and that Germany's future wars of national expansion would necessarily entail a war against the Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-A_History_of_Us:_War.2C_Peace_and_all_that_Jazz_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_History_of_Us:_War.2C_Peace_and_all_that_Jazz-64">[64]</a></sup> For Hitler and his principal lieutenants, national and racial issues were always dominant. This was symbolised by the adoption as the party emblem of the <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">swastika</a> or <i>Hakenkreuz</i>. In German nationalist circles, the swastika was considered a symbol of an "<a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan race</a>"; it symbolized the replacement of the Christian Cross with allegiance to a National Socialist State.</p>
<p>During 1921 and 1922, the Nazi Party grew significantly, partly through Hitler's oratorical skills, partly through the SA's appeal to unemployed young men, and partly because there was a backlash against socialist and liberal politics in Bavaria as Germany's economic problems deepened and the weakness of the Weimar regime became apparent. The party recruited former World War&#160;I soldiers, to whom Hitler as a decorated frontline veteran could particularly appeal, as well as small businessmen and disaffected former members of rival parties. Nazi rallies were often held in beer halls, where downtrodden men could get free beer. The <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a> was formed for the children of party members. The party also formed groups in other parts of Germany. <a href="/wiki/Julius_Streicher" title="Julius Streicher">Julius Streicher</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a> was an early recruit, and became editor of the racist magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Der_St%C3%BCrmer" title="Der Stürmer">Der Stürmer</a></i>. In December 1920 the Nazi Party had acquired a newspaper, the <i><a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkischer_Beobachter" title="Völkischer Beobachter">Völkischer Beobachter</a></i>, of which its leading ideologist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">Alfred Rosenberg</a> became editor. Others to join the party around this time were World War I flying ace <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a>.</p>
<p>On 31 October 1922, a party with similar policies and objectives came into power in Italy, the <a href="/wiki/National_Fascist_Party" title="National Fascist Party">National Fascist Party</a> under the leadership of the charismatic <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>. The Fascists, like the Nazis, promoted a national rebirth of their country; opposed communism and liberalism; appealed to the working-class; opposed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>; and advocated the territorial expansion of their country. The Italian Fascists used a straight-armed <a href="/wiki/Roman_salute" title="Roman salute">Roman salute</a> and wore black-shirted uniforms. Hitler was inspired by Mussolini and the Fascists, borrowing their use of the straight-armed salute as a Nazi salute. When the Fascists came to power in 1922 in Italy through their coup attempt called the "<a href="/wiki/March_on_Rome" title="March on Rome">March on Rome</a>", Hitler began planning his own coup.</p>
<p>In January 1923, France occupied the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr</a> industrial region as a result of Germany's failure to meet its <a href="/wiki/World_War_I_reparations" title="World War I reparations">reparations</a> payments. This led to economic chaos, the resignation of <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Cuno" title="Wilhelm Cuno">Wilhelm Cuno</a>'s government, and an attempt by the German Communist Party (KPD) to stage a revolution. The reaction to these events was an upsurge of nationalist sentiment. Nazi Party membership grew sharply, to about 20,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008110_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008110-65">[65]</a></sup> By November, Hitler had decided that the time was right for an attempt to seize power in Munich, in the hope that the <i>Reichswehr</i> (the post-war German military) would mutiny against the Berlin government and join his revolt. In this, he was influenced by former General <a href="/wiki/Erich_Ludendorff" title="Erich Ludendorff">Erich Ludendorff</a>, who had become a supporter—though not a member—of the Nazis.</p>
<p>On the night of 8 November, the Nazis used a patriotic rally in a Munich beer hall to launch an attempted <i>putsch</i> (<i>coup d'état</i>). This so-called <a href="/wiki/Beer_Hall_Putsch" title="Beer Hall Putsch">Beer Hall Putsch</a> attempt failed almost at once when the local <i>Reichswehr</i> commanders refused to support it. On the morning of 9 November the Nazis staged a march of about 2,000 supporters through Munich in an attempt to rally support. Troops opened fire, and 16 Nazis were killed. Hitler, Ludendorff and a number of others were arrested, and were tried for treason in March 1924. Hitler and his associates were given very lenient prison sentences. While Hitler was in prison, he wrote his semi-autobiographical political manifesto <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i> ("My Struggle").</p>
<p>The Nazi Party was banned on 9 November 1923, though with support of the nationalist <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch-Social_Bloc" title="Völkisch-Social Bloc">Völkisch-Social Bloc</a> (<i>Völkisch-Sozialer Block</i>), continued to operate under the name of the "German Party" (<i>Deutsche Partei</i> or DP) from 1924 to 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-dissolution_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dissolution-66">[66]</a></sup> The Nazis failed to remain unified in the German Party, as in the north, the right-wing <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch_movement" title="Völkisch movement">Volkish</a> nationalist supporters of the Nazis moved to the new <a href="/wiki/German_V%C3%B6lkisch_Freedom_Party" title="German Völkisch Freedom Party">German Völkisch Freedom Party</a>, leaving the north's left-wing Nazi members, such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a> retaining support for the party.<sup id="cite_ref-jablonsky_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jablonsky-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Rise_to_power:_1925–1933"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_to_power:_1925.E2.80.931933">Rise to power: 1925–1933</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>

</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289,_M%C3%BCnchen,_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289%2C_M%C3%BCnchen%2C_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg 2x" data-file-width="792" data-file-height="580" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-0289,_M%C3%BCnchen,_Hitler_bei_Einweihung_%22Braunes_Haus%22.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hitler with Nazi Party members in 1930</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler%27s_rise_to_power" title="Adolf Hitler's rise to power">Adolf Hitler's rise to power</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Rise of Nazism" redirects here. For the culmination of the rise, see <a href="/wiki/Nazi_seizure_of_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi seizure of power">Nazi seizure of power</a>.</div>
<p>Adolf Hitler was released from prison on 20 December 1924. On 16 February 1925 Hitler convinced the Bavarian authorities to lift the ban on the NSDAP, and the party was formally refounded on 26 February 1925, with Hitler as its undisputed Leader. The new Nazi Party was no longer a paramilitary organization, and disavowed any intention of taking power by force. In any case, the economic and political situation had stabilized and the extremist upsurge of 1923 had faded, so there was no prospect of further revolutionary adventures. The Nazi Party of 1925 was divided into the "Leadership Corps" (<i>Korps der politischen Leiter</i>), appointed by Hitler, and the general membership (<i>Parteimitglieder</i>). The party and the SA were kept separate, and the legal aspect of the party's work was emphasized. In a sign of this, the party began to admit women. The SA and the <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">SS</a> members (the latter founded in 1925 as Hitler's bodyguard, and known originally as the <i>Schutzkommando</i>) had to all be regular party members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeale201026.E2.80.9329_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeale201026.E2.80.9329-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoehl200434_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoehl200434-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1920s the Nazi Party expanded beyond its Bavarian base. Catholic Bavaria maintained its right-wing nostalgia for a Catholic monarch<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>; and <a href="/wiki/Westphalia" title="Westphalia">Westphalia</a>, along with working-class "Red Berlin", were always the Nazis' weakest areas electorally, even during the Third Reich itself. The areas of strongest Nazi support were in rural Protestant areas such as <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">Schleswig-Holstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mecklenburg" title="Mecklenburg">Mecklenburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomerania" title="Pomerania">Pomerania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a>. Depressed working-class areas such as <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a> also produced a strong Nazi vote, while the workers of the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> largely remained loyal to the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democrats</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">Communist Party of Germany</a>, or the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Centre_Party_(Germany)" title="Centre Party (Germany)">Centre Party</a>. Nuremberg remained a Nazi Party stronghold, and the first <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Rally" title="Nuremberg Rally">Nuremberg Rally</a> was held there in 1927. These rallies soon became massive displays of Nazi paramilitary power and attracted many recruits. The Nazis' strongest appeal was to the lower middle-classes&#160;– farmers, public servants, teachers, small businessmen&#160;– who had suffered most from the inflation of the 1920s, so who feared Bolshevism more than anything else. The small business class was receptive to Hitler's antisemitism, since it blamed Jewish big business for its economic problems. University students, disappointed at being too young to have served in the War of 1914–1918, and attracted by the Nazis' radical rhetoric, also became a strong Nazi constituency. By 1929, the party had 130,000 members.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008194_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008194-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The party's nominal Deputy Leader was <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Hess" title="Rudolf Hess">Rudolf Hess</a>, but he had no real power in the party. By the early 1930s the senior leaders of the party after Hitler were <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Himmler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Goebbels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Göring</a>. Beneath the Leadership Corps were the party's regional leaders, the <i><a href="/wiki/Gauleiter" title="Gauleiter">Gauleiters</a></i>, each of whom commanded the party in his <i><a href="/wiki/Gau_(country_subdivision)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gau (country subdivision)">Gau</a></i> ("region"). <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a> began his ascent through the party hierarchy as <i>Gauleiter</i> of Berlin-Brandenburg in 1926. Streicher was <i>Gauleiter</i> of <a href="/wiki/Franconia" title="Franconia">Franconia</a>, where he published his antisemitic newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Der_St%C3%BCrmer" title="Der Stürmer">Der Stürmer</a></i>. Beneath the <i>Gauleiter</i> were lower-level officials, the <i><a href="/wiki/Kreisleiter" title="Kreisleiter">Kreisleiter</a></i> ("county leaders"), <i><a href="/wiki/Zellenleiter" title="Zellenleiter">Zellenleiter</a></i> ("cell leaders") and <i><a href="/wiki/Blockleiter" title="Blockleiter">Blockleiter</a></i> ("block leaders"). This was a strictly hierarchical structure in which orders flowed from the top, and unquestioning loyalty was given to superiors. Only the SA retained some autonomy. Being composed largely of unemployed workers, many SA men took the Nazis' socialist rhetoric seriously. At this time, the <a href="/wiki/Hitler_salute" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitler salute">Hitler salute</a> (borrowed from the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Fascism" title="Italian Fascism">Italian fascists</a>) and the greeting "Heil Hitler!" were adopted throughout the party.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1930-election.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/06/1930-election.jpg/220px-1930-election.jpg" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/06/1930-election.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="293" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1930-election.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nazi Party election poster used in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> in 1930. Translation: "We demand freedom and bread".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Nazis contested elections to the national parliament, the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichstag_(Weimar_Republic)" title="Reichstag (Weimar Republic)">Reichstag</a></i>, and to the state legislatures, the <i><a href="/wiki/Landtag" title="Landtag">Landtage</a></i>, from 1924, although at first with little success. The "National-Socialist Freedom Movement" polled 3% of the vote in the <a href="/wiki/German_election,_December_1924" class="mw-redirect" title="German election, December 1924">December 1924 <i>Reichstag</i> elections</a>, and this fell to 2.6% in <a href="/wiki/German_election,_1928" class="mw-redirect" title="German election, 1928">1928</a>. State elections produced similar results. Despite these poor results, and despite Germany's relative political stability and prosperity during the later 1920s, the Nazi Party continued to grow. This was partly because Hitler, who had no administrative ability, left the party organization to the head of the secretariat, <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Bouhler" title="Philipp Bouhler">Philipp Bouhler</a>, the party treasurer <a href="/wiki/Franz_Xaver_Schwarz" title="Franz Xaver Schwarz">Franz Xaver Schwarz</a>, and business manager <a href="/wiki/Max_Amann" title="Max Amann">Max Amann</a>. The party had a capable propaganda head in <a href="/wiki/Gregor_Strasser" title="Gregor Strasser">Gregor Strasser</a>, who was promoted to national organizational leader in January 1928. These men gave the party efficient recruitment and organizational structures. The party also owed its growth to the gradual fading away of competitor nationalist groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/German_National_People%27s_Party" title="German National People's Party">German National People's Party</a> (DNVP). As Hitler became the recognized head of the German nationalists, other groups declined or were absorbed.</p>
<p>Despite these strengths, the Nazi Party might never have come to power had it not been for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> and its effects on Germany. By 1930 the German economy was beset with mass unemployment and widespread business failures. The Social Democrats and Communists were bitterly divided and unable to formulate an effective solution: this gave the Nazis their opportunity, and Hitler's message, blaming the crisis on the Jewish financiers and the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolsheviks</a>, resonated with wide sections of the electorate. At the <a href="/wiki/German_election,_1930" class="mw-redirect" title="German election, 1930">September 1930 <i>Reichstag</i> elections</a>, the Nazis won 18.3% of the votes and became the second-largest party in the <i>Reichstag</i> after the SPD. Hitler proved to be a highly effective campaigner, pioneering the use of radio and aircraft for this purpose. His dismissal of Strasser and his appointment of Goebbels as the party's propaganda chief were major factors. While Strasser had used his position to promote his own leftish version of national socialism, Goebbels was totally loyal to Hitler and worked only to improve Hitler's image.</p>
<p>The 1930 elections changed the German political landscape by weakening the traditional nationalist parties, the DNVP and the DVP, leaving the Nazis as the chief alternative to the discredited SPD and the Zentrum, whose leader, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Br%C3%BCning" title="Heinrich Brüning">Heinrich Brüning</a>, headed a weak minority government. The inability of the democratic parties to form a united front, the self-imposed isolation of the Communists, and the continued decline of the economy, all played into Hitler's hands. He now came to be seen as <i>de facto</i> leader of the opposition, and donations poured into the Nazi Party's coffers. Some major business figures, such as <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Thyssen" title="Fritz Thyssen">Fritz Thyssen</a>, were Nazi supporters and gave generously,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005372_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005372-71">[71]</a></sup> and some Wall Street figures were allegedly involved,<sup id="cite_ref-reformed-theology_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reformed-theology-72">[72]</a></sup> but many other businessmen were suspicious of the extreme nationalist tendencies of the Nazis and preferred to support the traditional conservative parties instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008224_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008224-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag,_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag%2C_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg/220px-Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag%2C_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag%2C_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg/330px-Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag%2C_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag%2C_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg/440px-Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag%2C_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3700" data-file-height="1800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wahl_zum_Preussischen_Landtag,_NSDAP-Wahlspendenmedaille_1932.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German NSDAP Donation Token 1932, Free State of Prussia Elections.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During 1931 and into 1932, Germany's political crisis deepened. In March 1932 Hitler ran for President against the incumbent President <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a>, polling 30.1% in the first round and 36.8% in the second against Hindenburg's 49 and 53%. By now the SA had 400,000 members, and its running street battles with the SPD and Communist paramilitaries (who also fought each other) reduced some German cities to combat zones. Paradoxically, although the Nazis were among the main instigators of this disorder, part of Hitler's appeal to a frightened and demoralised middle class was his promise to restore law and order. Overt antisemitism was played down in official Nazi rhetoric, but was never far from the surface. Germans voted for Hitler primarily because of his promises to revive the economy (by unspecified means), to restore German greatness and overturn the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>, and to save Germany from communism.</p>
<p>On 24 April 1932 the <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_Free_State_of_Prussia" title="Elections in the Free State of Prussia">Free State of Prussia elections</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Landtag_of_Prussia" title="Landtag of Prussia">Landtag</a> resulted in 36.3% of the votes, or 162 seats for Hitler's party NSDAP.</p>
<p>On 20 July 1932, the Prussian government was ousted by a coup, the <i>Preussenschlag</i>, and a few days later at the <a href="/wiki/German_election,_July_1932" class="mw-redirect" title="German election, July 1932">July 1932 <i>Reichstag</i> election</a> the Nazis made another leap forward, polling 37.4% and becoming the largest party in parliament by a wide margin. Furthermore, the Nazis and the Communists between them won 52% of the vote and a majority of seats. Since both parties opposed the established political system, and neither would join or support any ministry, this made the formation of a majority government impossible. The result was weak ministries governing by decree. Under <a href="/wiki/Comintern" class="mw-redirect" title="Comintern">Comintern</a> directives, the Communists maintained their policy of treating the SPD as the main enemy, calling them "<a href="/wiki/Social_fascism" title="Social fascism">social fascists</a>", thereby splintering opposition to the Nazis.<sup id="cite_ref-correspondence_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-correspondence-74">[74]</a></sup> Later, both the SPD and the Communists accused each other of having facilitated <a href="/wiki/Hitler%27s_rise_to_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitler's rise to power">Hitler's rise to power</a> by their unwillingness to compromise.</p>
<p>Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Papen" title="Franz von Papen">Franz von Papen</a> called another <i>Reichstag</i> election in November, hoping to find a way out of this impasse. The electoral result was the same, with the Nazis and the Communists winning 50% of the vote between them and more than half the seats, rendering this <i>Reichstag</i> no more workable than its predecessor. But support for the Nazis had fallen to 33.1%, suggesting that the Nazi surge had passed its peak&#160;– possibly because the worst of the Depression had passed, possibly because some middle-class voters had supported Hitler in July as a protest, but had now drawn back from the prospect of actually putting him into power. The Nazis interpreted the result as a warning that they must seize power before their moment passed. Had the other parties united, this could have been prevented, but their shortsightedness made a united front impossible. Papen, his successor <a href="/wiki/Kurt_von_Schleicher" title="Kurt von Schleicher">Kurt von Schleicher</a>, and the nationalist press magnate <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hugenberg" title="Alfred Hugenberg">Alfred Hugenberg</a> spent December and January in political intrigues that eventually persuaded President Hindenburg that it was safe to appoint Hitler as Reich Chancellor, at the head of a cabinet including only a minority of Nazi ministers—which he did on 30 January 1933.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascension_and_consolidation">Ascension and consolidation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A,_N%C3%BCrnberg,_Reichsparteitag,_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg/200px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg" width="200" height="348" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg/300px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg/400px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1982-159-21A,_N%C3%BCrnberg,_Reichsparteitag,_Hitler_und_R%C3%B6hm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Reichsparteitag</i> (Nuremberg Rally): Nazi Party leader Adolf Hitler and SA-leader Ernst Röhm, August 1933.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i>, Hitler directly attacked both left-wing and right-wing politics in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-politicians_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-politicians-75">[75]</a></sup> However, a majority of scholars identify <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> in practice as being a <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> form of politics.<sup id="cite_ref-Fritzsche.2C_Peter_1998_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fritzsche.2C_Peter_1998-76">[76]</a></sup> When asked in an interview in 1934 whether the Nazis were "bourgeois right-wing" as alleged by their opponents, Hitler responded that Nazism was not exclusively for any class, and indicated that it favoured neither the left nor the right, but preserved "pure" elements from both "camps", stating: "From the camp of bourgeois tradition, it takes national resolve, and from the materialism of the Marxist dogma, living, creative Socialism."<sup id="cite_ref-commentary_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commentary-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The votes that the Nazis received in the 1932 elections established the Nazi Party as the largest parliamentary faction of the Weimar Republic government. Adolf Hitler was appointed as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany_(German_Reich)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancellor of Germany (German Reich)">Chancellor of Germany</a> on 30 January 1933.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_fire" title="Reichstag fire"><i>Reichstag</i> fire</a> on 27 February 1933 gave Hitler a pretext for suppressing his political opponents. The following day, 28 February, he persuaded Reich's President <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a> to issue the <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_Fire_Decree" title="Reichstag Fire Decree"><i>Reichstag</i> Fire Decree</a>, which suspended most <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a>. The NSDAP won the <a href="/wiki/German_federal_election,_March_1933" title="German federal election, March 1933">parliamentary election on 5 March 1933</a> with 43.9 percent of votes, but failed to win an absolute majority. After the election, hundreds of thousands of new members joined the party for opportunistic reasons, most of them civil servants and white-collar workers. They were nicknamed the "casualties of March" (German: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Märzgefallenen</i></span>) or "March violets" (German: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Märzveilchen</i></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> To protect the party from too many non-ideological turncoats who were viewed by the so-called "old fighters" <i>(alte Kämpfer)</i> with some mistrust,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> the party issued a freeze on admissions that remained in force from May 1933 to 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 23 March, the parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Enabling_Act_of_1933" title="Enabling Act of 1933">Enabling Act of 1933</a>, which gave the cabinet the right to enact laws without the consent of parliament. In effect, this gave Hitler dictatorial powers. Now possessing virtually absolute power, the Nazis established <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarian</a> control; they abolished labour unions and other political parties and imprisoned their political opponents, first at <i>wilde Lager</i>, improvised camps, then in <a href="/wiki/Concentration_camp" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentration camp">concentration camps</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> had been established, yet the <i>Reichswehr</i> remained impartial: Nazi power over Germany remained virtual, not absolute.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_taking_power:_intertwining_of_party_and_state">After taking power: intertwining of party and state</span></h3>
<p>During June and July 1933 all competing parties were either outlawed or dissolved themselves. Subsequently, the <i>Law against the founding of new parties</i> of 14 July 1933 legally established the Nazi Party's monopoly. On 1 December 1933, the <i>Law to secure the unity of party and state</i> entered into force, which was the base for a progressive intertwining of party structures and state apparatus.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> By this law, the SA – actually a party division – was given quasi-governmental authority and their leader was co-opted as an <i>ex officio</i> cabinet member. By virtue of 30 January 1934 <i>Law about the reorganisation of the</i> Reich, the <i>Länder</i> (states) lost their statehood and were demoted to administrative divisions of the <i>Reich'</i>s government <i>(<a href="/wiki/Gleichschaltung" title="Gleichschaltung">Gleichschaltung</a>)</i>. Effectively, they lost most of their power to the <i><a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Nazi_Germany" title="Administrative divisions of Nazi Germany">Gaue</a></i>, that were originally just regional divisions of the party, but took over most competencies of the state administration in their respective sectors.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives_(1934)" class="mw-redirect" title="Night of the Long Knives (1934)">Röhm Purge</a> of 30 June to 2 July 1934 (also known as the "Night of the Long Knives"), Hitler disempowered the SA's leadership, most of whom belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Strasserism" title="Strasserism">Strasserist</a> (national revolutionary) faction within the NSDAP, and ordered them killed. He accused them of having conspired to stage a coup d'état, but it is believed that this was only a pretence to justify the suppression of any intraparty opposition. The purge was executed by the SS, assisted by the Gestapo and Reichswehr units. Aside from Strasserist Nazis, they also murdered anti-Nazi conservative figures like former chancellor <a href="/wiki/Kurt_von_Schleicher" title="Kurt von Schleicher">Kurt von Schleicher</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> After this, the SA continued to exist, but lost much of its importance, while the role of the SS grew significantly. Formerly only a sub-organisation of the SA, it was created a separate organisation of the NSDAP in July 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the death of President Hindenburg on 2 August 1934, Hitler merged the offices of party leader, head of state and chief of government in one, taking the title of <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer</a> und Reichskanzler</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Hitler%27s_Chancellery" title="Hitler's Chancellery">Chancellery of the Führer</a>, officially an organisation of the Nazi Party, took over the functions of the Office of the President (a government agency), blurring the distinction between structures of party and state even further. The SS increasingly exerted police functions, a development which was formally documented by the merger of the offices of <i><a href="/wiki/Reichsf%C3%BChrer-SS" title="Reichsführer-SS">Reichsführer-SS</a></i> and Chief of the German Police on 17 June 1936; the position was held by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> who derived his authority directly from Hitler.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Sicherheitsdienst" title="Sicherheitsdienst">Sicherheitsdienst</a></i> (SD, formally the "Security Service of the Reichsführer-SS") that had been created in 1931 as an intraparty intelligence became the de facto intelligence agency of Nazi Germany. It was put under the <a href="/wiki/Reich_Main_Security_Office" title="Reich Main Security Office">Reich Main Security Office</a> (RSHA) in 1939, which then coordinated SD, Gestapo and <a href="/wiki/Kriminalpolizei" title="Kriminalpolizei">criminal police</a>; therefore functioning as a hybrid organisation of state and party structures.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
