<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defeat_and_abolition">Defeat and abolition</span></h3>
<p>Officially, the Third Reich lasted only 12 years. The first <a href="/wiki/German_Instrument_of_Surrender" title="German Instrument of Surrender">Instrument of Surrender</a> was signed by representatives of Nazi Germany at <a href="/wiki/Reims" title="Reims">Reims</a>, France on 7 May 1945. The war in Europe had come to an end. The defeat of Germany in World War II marked the end of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> era.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNab200925_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNab200925-88">[88]</a></sup> The party was formally abolished on 10 October 1945 by the <a href="/wiki/Allied_Control_Council" title="Allied Control Council">Allied Control Council</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denazification" title="Denazification">denazification</a> began, along with <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">trials of major war criminals</a> before the International Military Tribunal (IMT) in Nuremberg.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNab200925.2C_26_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNab200925.2C_26-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_program">Political program</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Program" title="National Socialist Program">National Socialist Program</a></div>
<p>The National Socialist Program was a formulation of the policies of the party. It contained 25 points and is, therefore, also known as the "25-point plan" or "25-point programme". It was the official party programme, with minor changes, from its proclamation as such by Hitler in 1920, when the party was still the German Workers' Party, until its dissolution.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_composition">Party composition</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Command_structure">Command structure</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Top_leadership">Top leadership</span></h4>
<p>At the top of the Nazi Party was the party chairman ("<i>Der Führer</i>"), who held absolute power and full command over the party. All other party offices were subordinate to his position and had to depend on his instructions. In 1934, Hitler founded a separate body for the chairman, <a href="/wiki/Hitler%27s_Chancellery_(Kanzlei_des_F%C3%BChrers)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitler's Chancellery (Kanzlei des Führers)">Chancellery of the Führer</a>, with its own sub-units.</p>
<p>Below the Führer's chancellery was first the "Staff of the Deputy Führer", headed by <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Hess" title="Rudolf Hess">Rudolf Hess</a> from 21 April 1933 to 10 May 1941, and then the "Party Chancellery" (<i><a href="/wiki/Parteikanzlei" class="mw-redirect" title="Parteikanzlei">Parteikanzlei</a></i>) headed by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bormann" title="Martin Bormann">Martin Bormann</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reichsleiter">Reichsleiter</span></h4>
<p>Directly subjected to the Führer were the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichsleiter" title="Reichsleiter">Reichsleiter</a></i> ("Reich Leader(s)"—the singular and plural forms are identical in German), whose number was gradually increased to eighteen. They held power and influence comparable to the Reich Ministers' in <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Hitler" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabinet Hitler">Hitler's Cabinet</a>. The eighteen <i>Reichsleiter</i> formed the "Reich Leadership of the Nazi Party" (<i>Reichsleitung der NSDAP</i>), which was established at the so-called <a href="/wiki/Brown_House,_Munich,_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Brown House, Munich, Germany">Brown House</a>, in Munich. Unlike a Gauleiter, a Reichsleiter did not have individual geographic areas under their command, but were responsible for specific spheres of interest.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_leadership_corps">Political leadership corps</span></h4>
<p>The political leadership corps of the Nazi Party were those persons who were most often associated as being "Nazis" in the stereotypical sense of the word, as it was these individuals who wore brown paramilitary Nazi uniforms, enforced Nazi doctrine, and ran local government affairs in accordance with instructions from the Nazi Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZentnerBed.C3.BCrftig1997631_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZentnerBed.C3.BCrftig1997631-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The political leadership corps encompassed a vast array of <a href="/wiki/Ranks_and_insignia_of_the_Nazi_Party" title="Ranks and insignia of the Nazi Party">paramilitary titles</a> at the top of which were <a href="/wiki/Gauleiter" title="Gauleiter">Gauleiter</a>, who were Party leaders of large geographical areas. From the Gauleiters extended downwards through Nazi positions encompassing county, city, and town leaders, all of whom were unquestioned rulers in their particular areas and regions.<sup id="cite_ref-uniformen_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uniformen-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>To the very end of its existence, the Nazi Party claimed to respect the traditional government of Germany and, to that end, local and state governments were allowed to exist side-by-side with regional Nazi leaders. However, by 1936, the local governments had lost nearly all power to their Nazi counterparts or were now controlled by persons who held both government and Nazi titles alike. This led to the continued existence of German titles such as <i><a href="/wiki/Burgomaster" title="Burgomaster">Bürgermeister</a></i>, as well as the existence of German state legislatures (<i><a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">Landesrat</a></i>), but without any real power to speak of.<sup id="cite_ref-macmillan8_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-macmillan8-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordinary_members">Ordinary members</span></h4>
<p>The general Nazi Party membership were known by the title of <i>Parteimitglieder</i>. This generic term applied to any member of the Party who did not otherwise hold a political leadership position. Translated simply as "Party Member", the <i>Parteimitglieder</i> could (and did) hold positions in other Nazi groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/SS" class="mw-redirect" title="SS">SS</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a>. The only insignia for the <i>Parteimitglieder</i> was a Nazi Party lapel-pin; Nazi Party members who held no leadership posts had no specific designated uniform. Such persons often wore uniforms of other Nazi groups, uniforms of German government agencies, and could also serve in the German armed forces. A special designation of <i>Parteigenosse</i> (Party Comrade) was reserved for Nazi Party members who had been members of the party since the 1920s and early 30s, most of whom were also personal associates of Hitler.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nazi_Party_offices">Nazi Party offices</span></h3>
<p>The Nazi Party had a number of party offices dealing with various political and other matters. These included:</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/NSDAP_Office_of_Racial_Policy" title="NSDAP Office of Racial Policy">Rassenpolitisches Amt der NSDAP</a></i> (RPA): "NSDAP Office of Racial Policy"</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/NSDAP_Office_of_Foreign_Affairs" title="NSDAP Office of Foreign Affairs">Außenpolitische Amt der NSDAP</a></i> (APA): "NSDAP Office of Foreign Affairs"</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/NSDAP_Office_of_Colonial_Policy" title="NSDAP Office of Colonial Policy">Kolonialpolitisches Amt der NSDAP</a></i> (KPA): "NSDAP Office of Colonial Policy"</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/NSDAP_Office_of_Military_Policy" title="NSDAP Office of Military Policy">Wehrpolitisches Amt der NSDAP</a></i> (WPA): "NSDAP Office of Military Policy"</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Amt_Rosenberg" title="Amt Rosenberg">Amt Rosenberg</a></i> (ARo): "<a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">Rosenberg</a> Office"</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paramilitary_groups">Paramilitary groups</span></h3>
<p>In addition to the Nazi Party proper, several paramilitary groups existed which "supported" Nazi aims. All members of these paramilitary organizations were required to become regular Nazi Party members first and could then enlist in the group of their choice. An exception was the <a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a>, considered the military arm of the SS and Nazi Party, which during the Second World War allowed members to enlist without joining the Nazi Party. Foreign volunteers of the Waffen-SS were also not required to be members of the Nazi Party, although many joined local nationalist groups from their own countries with the same aims. Police officers, including members of the <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a>, frequently held SS rank for administrative reasons (known as "rank parity") and were likewise not required to be members of the Nazi Party.</p>
<p>A vast system of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party_paramilitary_ranks" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi Party paramilitary ranks">Nazi Party paramilitary ranks</a> developed for each of the various paramilitary groups.</p>
<p>The major Nazi Party paramilitary groups were as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">Schutzstaffel</a></i> (SS): "Protection Squadron" (both <a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_SS" title="Allgemeine SS">Allgemeine SS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a></i> (SA): "Storm Division"</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Flyers_Corps" title="National Socialist Flyers Corps">Nationalsozialistisches Fliegerkorps</a></i> (NSFK): "National Socialist Flyers Corps"</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Motor_Corps" title="National Socialist Motor Corps">Nationalsozialistisches Kraftfahrerkorps</a></i> (NSKK): "National Socialist Motor Corps"</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a> was a paramilitary group divided into an adult leadership corps and a general membership open to boys aged fourteen to eighteen. The <a href="/wiki/League_of_German_Girls" title="League of German Girls">League of German Girls</a> was the equivalent group for girls.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Affiliated_organizations">Affiliated organizations</span></h3>
<p>Certain nominally independent organizations had their own legal representation and own property, but were supported by the Nazi Party. Many of these associated organizations were labour unions of various professions. Some were older organizations that were nazified according to the <i>Gleichschaltung</i> policy after the 1933 takeover.</p>
<ul>
<li>Reich League of German Officials (union of civil servants, predecessor to <a href="/wiki/German_Civil_Service_Federation" title="German Civil Service Federation">German Civil Service Federation</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/German_Labour_Front" title="German Labour Front">German Labour Front</a> (DAF)</li>
<li>National Socialist German Physicians' League (NSDÄB)</li>
<li>National Socialist League for the Maintenance of the Law (NSRB, 1936–1945, earlier National Socialist German Lawyers' League)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/NSKOV" class="mw-redirect" title="NSKOV">National Socialist War Victim's Care</a> (NSKOV)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Teachers_League" title="National Socialist Teachers League">National Socialist Teachers League</a> (NSLB)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_People%27s_Welfare" title="National Socialist People's Welfare">National Socialist People's Welfare</a> (NSV)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reich_Labour_Service" title="Reich Labour Service">Reich Labour Service</a> (RAD)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/German_Faith_Movement" title="German Faith Movement">German Faith Movement</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/German_Colonial_League" class="mw-redirect" title="German Colonial League">German Colonial League</a> (RKB)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/German_Red_Cross" title="German Red Cross">German Red Cross</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kyffh%C3%A4userbund" title="Kyffhäuserbund">Kyffhäuser League</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/TENO" class="mw-redirect" title="TENO">Technical Emergency Relief</a> (TENO)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reichsbund_der_Kinderreichen" title="Reichsbund der Kinderreichen">Reich's Union of Large Families</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reichsluftschutzbund" title="Reichsluftschutzbund">Reichsluftschutzbund</a> (RLB)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Reichskolonialbund" title="Reichskolonialbund">Reichskolonialbund</a> (RKB)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bund_Deutscher_Osten" title="Bund Deutscher Osten">Bund Deutscher Osten</a> (BDO)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/German_American_Bund" title="German American Bund">German American Bund</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_administration">Regional administration</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nsdap_gaue_english.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Nsdap_gaue_english.png/200px-Nsdap_gaue_english.png" width="200" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Nsdap_gaue_english.png/300px-Nsdap_gaue_english.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Nsdap_gaue_english.png/400px-Nsdap_gaue_english.png 2x" data-file-width="8881" data-file-height="5315" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nsdap_gaue_english.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Party Gaue in 1926, 1928, 1933, 1937, 1939 and 1943</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png/200px-NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png" width="200" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png/300px-NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png/400px-NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png 2x" data-file-width="13039" data-file-height="9936" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NS_administrative_Gliederung_1944.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Administrative units of the Nazi Party in 1944.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Nazi_Germany" title="Administrative divisions of Nazi Germany">Administrative divisions of Nazi Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Gauleiters" title="List of Gauleiters">List of Gauleiters</a></div>
<p>For the purpose of centralization in the <i><a href="/wiki/Gleichschaltung" title="Gleichschaltung">Gleichschaltung</a></i> process a rigidly hierarchal structure was established in the Nazi Party, which it later carried through in the whole of Germany in order consolidate total power under the person of <a href="/wiki/Hitler" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitler">Hitler</a> (<i>Führerstaat</i>). It was regionally sub-divided into a number of <i><a href="/wiki/Gau_(country_subdivision)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gau (country subdivision)">Gaue</a></i> (singular: <i>Gau</i>) headed by a <i><a href="/wiki/Gauleiter" title="Gauleiter">Gauleiter</a></i>, who received their orders directly from Hitler. The name (originally a term for sub-regions of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> headed by a <i>Gaugraf</i>) for these new provincial structures was deliberately chosen because of its <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">mediaeval</a> connotations. The term is approximately equivalent to the English <i><a href="/wiki/Shire" title="Shire">shire</a></i>.</p>
<p>After the <i><a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a></i> a new type of administrative unit was introduced called a <i><a href="/wiki/Reichsgau" title="Reichsgau">Reichsgau</a></i>. In these territories the Gauleiters also held the position of <a href="/wiki/Reichsstatthalter" title="Reichsstatthalter">Reichsstatthalter</a>, thereby formally combining the spheres of both party and state offices. The establishment of this type of district was subsequently carried out for any further territorial annexations of Germany both before and during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.</p>
<p>The <i>Gaue</i> and <i>Reichsgaue</i> (state or province) were further sub-divided into <i><a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Germany" title="Districts of Germany">Kreise</a></i> (counties) headed by a <i>Kreisleiter</i>, which were in turn sub-divided into <i>Zellen</i> (cells) and <i>Blocken</i> (blocks), headed by a <i>Zellenleiter</i> and <i>Blockleiter</i> respectively.</p>
<p>A reorganization of the <i>Gaue</i> was enacted on 1 October 1928. The given numbers were the official ordering numbers. The statistics are from 1941, for which the <i>Gau</i> organization of that moment in time forms the basis. Their size and populations are not exact; for instance according to the official party statistics the <i>Gau</i> Kurmark/Mark Brandenburg was the largest in the German Reich.<sup id="cite_ref-google_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-93">[93]</a></sup> By 1941, there were 42 territorial <i>Gaue</i> for Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> 7 of them for Austria, the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a> (in <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a>), <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Danzig" title="Free City of Danzig">Danzig</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Saar_(League_of_Nations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Saar (League of Nations)">Territory of the Saar Basin</a>, along with the unincorporated regions under German control known as the Protectorate of Bohemia-Moravia and the <a href="/wiki/General_Government" title="General Government">General Government</a>, established after the joint <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a> by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in 1939 at the onset of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GHI2008_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GHI2008-95">[95]</a></sup> Getting the leadership of the individual <i>Gaue</i> to cooperate with one another proved difficult at times since there was constant administrative and financial jockeying for control going on between them.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The table below uses the organizational structure that existed before its dissolution in 1945. More information on the older <i>Gaue</i> is in the second table.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaue_dissolved_before_1945"><i>Gaue</i> dissolved before 1945</span></h3>
<p>Simple re-namings of existing <i>Gaue</i> without territorial changes is marked with the initials <i><b>RN</b></i> in the column "later became". The numbering is not based on any official former ranking, but merely listed alphabetically.</p>

<td style="background:#abcdef;"><b>later became</b></td>
<td style="background:#abcdef;"><b>… together with</b></td>
<td style="background:#abcdef;"><b>Gauleiter</b></td>
</tr>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Associated_organizations_abroad">Associated organizations abroad</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/NSDAP/AO" title="NSDAP/AO">NSDAP/AO</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaue_in_Switzerland"><i>Gaue</i> in Switzerland</span></h4>
<p>The irregular Swiss branch of the Nazi Party also established a number of Party <i>Gaue</i> in that country, most of them named after their regional capitals. These included <i>Gau <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Basel" title="Canton of Basel">Basel</a>-<a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Solothurn" title="Canton of Solothurn">Solothurn</a></i>, <i>Gau <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Schaffhausen" title="Canton of Schaffhausen">Schaffhausen</a></i>, <i>Gau <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Luzern" class="mw-redirect" title="Canton of Luzern">Luzern</a></i>, <i>Gau <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Bern" title="Canton of Bern">Bern</a></i> and <i>Gau <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Z%C3%BCrich" title="Canton of Zürich">Zürich</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-google9_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google9-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Examples_of_NSDAP_and_National_Front_meetings_and_agendas_in_northern_Switzerland.2C_1935.2C_1937_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Examples_of_NSDAP_and_National_Front_meetings_and_agendas_in_northern_Switzerland.2C_1935.2C_1937-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Archiv_des_Historischen_Vereins_des_Kantons_Bern.2C_vol_57.E2.80.9360_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Archiv_des_Historischen_Vereins_des_Kantons_Bern.2C_vol_57.E2.80.9360-99">[99]</a></sup> The <i>Gau Ostschweiz</i> (East Switzerland) combined the territories of three cantons: <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_St._Gallen" title="Canton of St. Gallen">St. Gallen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Thurgau" class="mw-redirect" title="Canton of Thurgau">Thurgau</a> und <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Appenzell" class="mw-redirect" title="Canton of Appenzell">Appenzell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Die_Nationale_front_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Die_Nationale_front-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_membership">General membership</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg/220px-Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg" width="220" height="385" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg/330px-Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg/440px-Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1004" data-file-height="1756" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parteibuch_der_NSDAP.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Membership of the Nazi Party from 1939.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nazi_Party_members" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Nazi Party members">List of Nazi Party members</a></div>
<p>The general membership of the Nazi Party mainly consisted of the urban and rural <a href="/wiki/Lower_middle_class" title="Lower middle class">lower middle classes</a>. 7% belonged to the upper class, another 7% were <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a>, 35% were industrial workers and 51% were what can be described as middle class. In early 1933, just before Hitler's appointment to the chancellorship, the party showed an under-representation of "workers", who made up 29.7% of the membership but 46.3% of German society. Conversely, white-collar employees (18.6% of members and 12% of Germans), the self-employed (19.8% of members and 9.6% of Germans), and civil servants (15.2% of members and 4.8% of the German population) had joined in proportions greater than their share of the general population.<sup id="cite_ref-Panayi_2007.2C_p._40_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panayi_2007.2C_p._40-101">[101]</a></sup> These members were affiliated with local branches of the party, of which there were 1,378 throughout the country in 1928. In 1932, the number had risen to 11,845, reflecting the party's growth in this period.<sup id="cite_ref-Panayi_2007.2C_p._40_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Panayi_2007.2C_p._40-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>When it came to power in 1933, the Nazi Party had over <span class="nowrap">2 million</span> members. In 1939, the membership total rose to 5.3&#160;million with 81% being male and 19% being female. It continued to attract many more and by 1945 the party reached its peak of 8 million with 63% being male and 37% being female (about 10% of the German population of 80&#160;million).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNab201122.2C_23_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNab201122.2C_23-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Population_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Population-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_membership">Military membership</span></h3>
<p>Nazi members with military ambitions were encouraged to join the <i><a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a></i>, but a great number enlisted in the <i>Wehrmacht</i> and even more were drafted for service after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War&#160;II</a> began. Early regulations required that all <i>Wehrmacht</i> members be non-political, and any Nazi member joining in the 1930s was required to resign from the Nazi Party.</p>
<p>This regulation was soon waived, however, and there is ample evidence that full Nazi Party members served in the <i>Wehrmacht</i> in particular after the outbreak of World War&#160;II. The <i>Wehrmacht</i> Reserves also saw a high number of senior Nazis enlisting, with <a href="/wiki/Reinhard_Heydrich" title="Reinhard Heydrich">Reinhard Heydrich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Todt" title="Fritz Todt">Fritz Todt</a> joining the <i><a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a></i>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Karl_Hanke" title="Karl Hanke">Karl Hanke</a> who served in the army.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Student_membership">Student membership</span></h3>
<p>In 1926, the party formed a special division to engage the student population, known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_German_Students%27_League" title="National Socialist German Students' League">National Socialist German Students' League</a> (NSDStB). A group for university lecturers, the <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_German_University_Lecturers%27_League" class="mw-redirect" title="National Socialist German University Lecturers' League">National Socialist German University Lecturers' League</a> (NSDDB), also existed until July 1944.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_membership">Women membership</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/NS-Frauenschaft" class="mw-redirect" title="NS-Frauenschaft">National Socialist Women's League</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_wing" title="Women's wing">women's organization</a> of the party. By 1938 it had approximately 2 million members.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership_outside_Germany">Membership outside Germany</span></h3>
<p>Party members who lived outside Germany were pooled into the <i>Auslands-Organisation</i> (<a href="/wiki/NSDAP/AO" title="NSDAP/AO">NSDAP/AO</a>, "Foreign Organization"). The organization was limited only to so-called "<a href="/wiki/Imperial_Germans" title="Imperial Germans">Imperial Germans</a>"; "Ethnic Germans" (<i><a href="/wiki/Volksdeutsche" title="Volksdeutsche">Volksdeutsche</a></i>) who did not hold German citizenship were not permitted to join.</p>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Bene%C5%A1_decrees" title="Beneš decrees">Beneš decree</a> <a href="/wiki/Bene%C5%A1_decrees#List_of_decrees" title="Beneš decrees">No. 16/1945 Coll.</a>, in case of citizens of Czechoslovakia, membership of the Nazi Party was punishable by between five and twenty years of imprisonment.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deutsche_Gemeinschaft"><i>Deutsche Gemeinschaft</i></span></h4>
<p><i>Deutsche Gemeinschaft</i> was a branch of the Nazi Party founded in 1919, created for Germans with <i><a href="/wiki/Volksdeutsche" title="Volksdeutsche">Volksdeutsche</a></i> status.<sup id="cite_ref-Fakty_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fakty-103">[103]</a></sup> It is not to be confused with the post-war right-wing <i><span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Gemeinschaft" class="mw-redirect" title="Deutsche Gemeinschaft">Deutsche Gemeinschaft</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deutsche_Gemeinschaft_(Deutschland)" class="extiw" title="de:Deutsche Gemeinschaft (Deutschland)">de</a>)</span></span></i> founded in 1949.</p>
<p>Notable members included:<sup id="cite_ref-gemeinschaft_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gemeinschaft-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oswald_Menghin" title="Oswald Menghin">Oswald Menghin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Czaja" title="Herbert Czaja">Herbert Czaja</a> (<a href="/wiki/Province_of_Silesia" title="Province of Silesia">Province of Silesia</a> inside <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hermann_Neubacher" title="Hermann Neubacher">Hermann Neubacher</a> who was responsible for invading Yugoslavia.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Much" title="Rudolf Much">Rudolf Much</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sey%C3%9F-Inquart" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur Seyß-Inquart">Arthur Seyß-Inquart</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_symbols">Party symbols</span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Germany" title="Flag of Germany">Nazi flags</a>: The Nazi Party used a right-facing <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">swastika</a> as their symbol and the red and black colours were said to represent <i><a href="/wiki/Blood_and_soil" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood and soil">Blut und Boden</a></i> ("blood and soil"). Another definition of the flag describes the colours as representing the ideology of National Socialism, the swastika representing the Aryan race and the Aryan nationalist agenda of the movement; white representing Aryan racial purity; and red representing the socialist agenda of the movement. Black, white and red were in fact the colours of the old <a href="/wiki/North_German_Confederation" title="North German Confederation">North German Confederation</a> flag (invented by <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a>, based on the Prussian colours black and white and the red used by northern German states). In 1871, with the foundation of the German Reich, the flag of the North German Confederation became the German <i>Reichsflagge</i> ("Reich flag"). Black, white and red became the colours of the nationalists through the following history (for example <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>).</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_NSDAP_(1920%E2%80%931945).svg" title="File:Flag of the NSDAP (1920–1945).svg"><i>Parteiflagge</i></a> design, with the centred swastika disc, served as the party flag from 1920. Between 1933 (when the Nazi Party came to power) and 1935, it was used as the National flag (<i>Nationalflagge</i>) and Merchant flag (<i>Handelsflagge</i>), but interchangeably with the <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_German_Reich_(1933%E2%80%931935).svg" title="File:Flag of German Reich (1933–1935).svg">black-white-red horizontal tricolour</a>. In 1935, the black-white-red horizontal tricolour was scrapped (again), and the <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_German_Reich_(1935%E2%80%931945).svg" title="File:Flag of German Reich (1935–1945).svg">flag with the <i>off-centre</i> swastika and disc</a> was instituted as the national flag, and remained as such until 1945. The flag with the <i>centred</i> disk continued to be used after 1935, but exclusively as the <i>Parteiflagge</i>, the flag of the party.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Germany" title="Coat of arms of Germany">German eagle</a>: The Nazi Party used the traditional <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Germany" title="Coat of arms of Germany">German eagle</a>, standing atop of a <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">swastika</a> inside a wreath of oak leaves. It is also known as the <i>Iron Eagle.</i> When the eagle is looking to its left shoulder, it symbolises the Nazi Party, and was called the <i>Parteiadler</i>. In contrast, when the eagle is looking to its right shoulder, it symbolises the country (<i><a href="/wiki/Reich" title="Reich">Reich</a></i>), and was therefore called the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichsadler" title="Reichsadler">Reichsadler</a></i>. After the Nazi Party came to national power in Germany, they replaced the traditional version of the German eagle with the modified party symbol throughout the country and all its institutions.</li>
</ul>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

<li id="cite_note-SZspeak-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-SZspeak_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">or <i>Sozialdemokrat</i> (pronounced /zo'tsjaːldemoˌkraːt/) (<a href="/wiki/Social_democracy" title="Social democracy">social democrat</a>).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Mautner-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Mautner_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mautner_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFFranz_H._Mautner1944" class="citation journal">Franz H.
